<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41597"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="An improved high-quality genome assembly and annotation of Tibetan hulless barley"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Data 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-05-08"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Hulless barley (Hordeum vulgare L. var. nudum) is a barley variety that has loose husk cover of the caryopses. Because of the ease in processing and edibility, hulless barley has been locally cultivated and used as human food. For example, in Tibetan Plateau, hulless barley is the staple food for human and essential livestock feed. Although the draft genome of hulless barley has been sequenced, the assembly remains fragmented. Here, we reported an improved high-quality assembly and annotation of the Tibetan hulless barley genome using more than 67X PacBio long-reads. The N50 contig length of the new assembly is at least more than 19 times larger than other available barley assemblies. The new genome assembly also showed high gene completeness and high collinearity of genome synteny with the previously reported barley genome. The new genome assembly and annotation will not only remove major hurdles in genetic analysis and breeding of hulless barley, but will also serve as a key resource for studying barley genomics and genetics. 
                
                  
                    
                    
                    
                  
                
               Machine-accessible metadata file describing the reported data: https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12162147"/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Hulless barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i> L. var. <i>nudum</i>) is a barley variety that has loose husk cover of the caryopses. Because of the ease in processing and edibility, hulless barley has been locally cultivated and used as human food. For example, in Tibetan Plateau, hulless barley is the staple food for human and essential livestock feed. Although the draft genome of hulless barley has been sequenced, the assembly remains fragmented. Here, we reported an improved high-quality assembly and annotation of the Tibetan hulless barley genome using more than 67X PacBio long-reads. The N50 contig length of the new assembly is at least more than 19 times larger than other available barley assemblies. The new genome assembly also showed high gene completeness and high collinearity of genome synteny with the previously reported barley genome. The new genome assembly and annotation will not only remove major hurdles in genetic analysis and breeding of hulless barley, but will also serve as a key resource for studying barley genomics and genetics.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Abs2" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content">
                <div class="c-article-table-container"><div class="c-article-table-border c-table-scroll-wrapper"><div class="c-table-scroll-wrapper__content" data-component-scroll-wrapper=""><table class="data last-table"><tbody><tr><td class="u-text-left ">

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background &amp; Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background &amp; Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Hulless barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i> L. var. <i>nudum</i>) is a monophyletically originated variety of barley that has loose husk cover of the caryopses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Taketa, S. et al. Monophyletic origin of naked barley inferred from molecular analyses of a marker closely linked to the naked caryopsis gene (nud). Theoretical and Applied Genetics 108, 1236â1242 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d42784e597">1</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Fig1">1</a>). While many domesticated barley varieties are hulled and are mainly used for brewing malt and animal feed, hulless barley has been cultivated on a small scale and used as human food because of the ease in processing and edibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Taketa, S. et al. Monophyletic origin of naked barley inferred from molecular analyses of a marker closely linked to the naked caryopsis gene (nud). Theoretical and Applied Genetics 108, 1236â1242 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d42784e604">1</a></sup>. Although the cultivation of hulless barley is widely distributed, the frequency decreases from east to west<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Zeng, X. et al. Origin and evolution of qingke barley in Tibet. Nature Communications 9, 5433 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d42784e609">2</a></sup>. The most frequently cultivated area is the Tibetan plateau, e.g. Nepal and Tibet, where hulless barley accounts for more than 95% of domesticated barley and is the staple food for people and an important livestock feed. Recently, hulless barley is also increasingly attracting attention as a potential crop for the development of value-added products and multiple food applications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Izydorczyk, M. S., Chornick, T. L., Paulley, F. G., Edwards, N. M. &amp; Dexter, J. E. Physicochemical properties of hull-less barley fibre-rich fractions varying in particle size and their potential as functional ingredients in two-layer flat bread. Journal of food science and technology 108, 561â570 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d42784e613">3</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0480-0/MediaObjects/41597_2020_480_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0480-0/MediaObjects/41597_2020_480_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="599" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Morphology of a Tibetan hulless barley. These pictures show (<b>a</b>) seedling; (<b>b</b>) heading stage; (<b>c</b>) mature stage; (<b>d</b>) filling stage spike; (<b>e</b>) grain of a hulless barley cultivated in Tibet.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Obtaining genomic sequences is critical for efficient molecular breeding and understanding of the evolutionary history of crops. Recently, studies, including one from our own group, have made significant progress in sequencing the genomes of hulless barley. Using the short-read sequencing approach, the genomes of two hulless barley strains that were grown in Tibet were sequenced and assembled<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e643">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Dai, F. et al. Assembly and analysis of a qingke reference genome demonstrate its close genetic relation to modern cultivated barley. Plant Biotechnology Journal 16, 760â770 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42784e646">5</a></sup>. The results suggest that many stress-related genes, which were expanded in hulless barley, might have facilitated the adaptation to the high-altitude environment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e650">4</a></sup> and may provide a useful genetic resource for improving barley. Furthermore, by sequencing a population of 437 accessions, a study also showed that the current Tibetan hulless barley cultivars were derived from eastern domesticated barley and were introduced to southern Tibet between 4,500 and 3,500 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Fu, D. X., Ruan, R. W., Dai, X. M. &amp; Liu, Y. M. A study on ancient barley, wheat and millet discovered at Changguo in Tibet. Acta Agronomica sinica 26, 392â398 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42784e654">6</a></sup>. However, due to its large genome size and rich in transposable element sequences (80.8â84%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e658">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Mayer, K. et al. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711â716 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42784e661">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427â433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42784e664">8</a></sup>, the genome assemblies of hulless barley using short-read sequencing approach remain incomplete and fragmented. This constrains the molecular breeding in hulless barley and the use of hulless barley for food applications.</p><p>Here, using a long-read sequencing technique (Pacific Biosciences), we sequenced a Tibetan hulless barley cultivar (Lasa Goumang) that has been previously sequenced using short-read, in high coverage (&gt;67X). Using both Pacbio long reads and available Illumina reads, we assembled a significantly improved genome, of which the N50 contig size reached ~1.56âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Tab1">1</a>). Based on this improved assembly, we re-annotated the protein-coding genes in hulless barley and anchored the scaffolds to a linkage map of the barley cv. Morex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). The Plant Journal 76, 718â727 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42784e674">9</a></sup>. The improved genome assembly and annotations will not only serve as a key resource for exploring the economic and genetic values of hulless barley varieties, but will also advance researches in barley genomics and genetics.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Titetan hulless barley genome size estimation and assembly statistics in previous study.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">DNA isolation, libraries construction and sequencing</h3><p>The seedlings were germinated from seeds of a Tibetan hulless barley (cultivar Lasa Goumang, NCBI BioSample ID SAMN09914874). Tissues were flash-frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored in the freezer until DNA extraction. DNA was extracted using the cetyltrimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Rogers, S. O. &amp; Bendich, A. J. In Plant Molecular Biology Manual (eds. Stanton B. Gelvin, Robbert A. Schilperoort &amp; Desh Pal S. Verma) 73â83 (Springer Netherlands, 1989)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d42784e765">10</a></sup>. The quality of the extracted genomic DNA was checked using electrophoresis on 1% agarose gel and the concentration was quantified using a Qubit fluorometer (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA, USA).</p><p>Single-molecule real-time (SMRT) long-reads sequencing was performed at NextOmics Technology Corporation (Wuhan, China) with a PacBio sequel sequencer (Pacific Biosciences, Menlo Park, CA, USA). The SMRT Bell library was prepared using a DNA Template Prep Kit (1.0). In total, six 20-kb SMRT Bell libraries were constructed. Genomic DNA (~10 Î¼g) was mechanically sheared to fragments of approximately 20âkb using a Covaris g-TUBE. The fragment size distribution was assessed using a bioanalyzer 2100 12âK DNA Chip assay. A blunt-end ligation reaction followed by exonuclease treatment was conducted to generate the SMRT Bell template. The size-selection of SMRT Bell templates was performed using a BluePippin size-selection system (Sage Science) to enrich large fragments (&gt;10âkb). The quality and quantity of size-selected libraries were assessed on a bioanalyzer12Kb DNA Chip (Agilent) and a Qubit fluorometer (Life Technologies), respectively. The SMRT bell libraries were prepared using the binding kit 2.0 (PacBio p/n 100-862-200) according to the manufacturerâs instructions. The libraries were sequenced using a PacBio Sequel instrument on PacBio SMRT cells v2.0 (Pacific Biosciences, acquiring one movie of 360âmin per SMRT cell). The MagBead loading (Pacific Biosciences) method was used to improve the enrichment of the larger fragments. In total, ~300âGb subreads sequences (average length: 9,358âbp) were generated on 64 SMRT cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">RNA isolation and Iso-Seq sequencing</h3><p>For RNA samples, plants were grown in a climate chamber in the laboratory (Lhasa). Roots, stems and leaves were sampled seven weeks after germination. To have sufficient materials for each RNA sample, we pooled plant tissue from 10 plants. Samples were placed on dry ice during sample collection and stored in â80âÂ°C freezer until RNA isolation. In total, five pooled samples (one root, two stem and two leaf samples) were collected. Samples were ground with liquid nitrogen and total RNA was extracted using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen) according to manufacturerâs protocol. RQ1 DNase (Promega) was used to remove DNA.</p><p>cDNA libraries were prepared using the ClontechSMARTer<sup>Â®</sup> cDNA synthesis kit according to the manufacturerâs recommendations. One Î¼g total RNA was used for each of the five samples. Barcoded oligo dT was used to barcode samples. The cDNA products were purified with AMPure PB beads and quality control (QC) was performed on BioAnalyzer 2100 (Agilent). The purified cDNA libraries were pooled in an equal molar ratio. The pooled cDNA (~3.8 Î¼g) was size fractionated using the Sage ELF system. Subsequent re-amplification was performed to yield four libraries (size of 1â2, 2â3, 3â6 and 5â10Kb) to minimize artifacts during large-scale amplification. The pooled PCR products were purified using AMPure PB beads. One to five Î¼g of purified amplicons were subjected to Iso-Seq SMRT Bell library preparation (<a href="https://pacbio.secure.force.com/SamplePrep">https://pacbio.secure.force.com/SamplePrep</a>). A total of 17 SMRT cells were sequenced on the PacBio RS II platform using P6-C4 chemistry with 3â4âh movies. In total, 19.68âGb sequence data (~1.5 million reads) were obtained (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Tab2">2</a>). The average subreads length was 4.0âkb and the average subreads quality was 0.9.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Iso-Seq library information and sequencing results.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assembly</h3><p>In our assembly workflow, raw bam files from PacBio Sequel were first converted into subreads in fasta format using the PacBio software BAM2fastx. Then we used the falcon package (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/falcon">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/falcon</a>) to construct the primary assembly. Error correction was performed using an overlap-based strategy and the error-corrected reads were used to construct the contigs (parameters: length_cutoffâ=â5000; length_cutoff_prâ=â10000). To correct errors in the primary assembly, we used the arrow pipeline from the SMRT link 4 toolkit to polish the genome (<a href="https://www.pacb.com/products-and-services/analytical-software/smrt-analysis/">https://www.pacb.com/products-and-services/analytical-software/smrt-analysis/</a>). The PacBio reads were aligned to the primary assembly using pbalign and variantCaller was used to call variants.</p><p>SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578â579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1079">11</a></sup> was used to construct scaffolds from contigs. First, we aligned previously sequenced Illumina mate-pair libraries (20âkb and 40Kb fragment long)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1083">4</a></sup> to the assembled contigs using bowtie v1.1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Langmead, B. Aligning short sequencing reads with Bowtie. Current protocols in bioinformatics, Chapter 11, Unit 11.7 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1087">12</a></sup> and constructed scaffolds with SSPACE-STANDARD-3.0. Second, we used PacBio long-reads to further improve the scaffolding using SSPACE-LongRead<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1091">13</a></sup>. After scaffolding, the assembly contains 1,856 scaffolds, with a N50 contig size of 1.56âMb and an N50 scaffold size of 4.0âMb. The assembled genome size is 4.0âGb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Comparison of the new genome with previously published assemblies of the Tibetan hulless barley genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>While the genome size of barley cv. Morex is ~5.1 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). The Plant Journal 76, 718â727 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1204">9</a></sup>, our previous work has suggested that the genome size of Tibetan hulless barley is ~4.5âGb using k-mer analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1208">4</a></sup> (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Tab1">1</a>). However, it is well-known that genome size estimation from both k-mer approach and flow cytometry can have substantial standard deviations (e.g., 10%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="He, K., Lin, K., Wang, G. &amp; Li, F. Genome sizes of nine insect species determined by flow cytometry and k-mer analysis. Frontiers in physiology 7, 569 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1215">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guo, L. T. et al. Flow cytometry and K-mer analysis estimates of the genome sizes of Bemisia tabaci B and Q (Hemiptera: Aleyrodidae). Frontiers in physiology 6, 144 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1218">15</a></sup>. To draw concrete conclusion on genome size differences between hulless barley and cv. Morex, additional experiments are required. However, this is beyond the scope of this study.</p><p>We further generated pseudochromosomes using the assembled scaffolds and linkage map of barley cv. Morex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). The Plant Journal 76, 718â727 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1225">9</a></sup>. We used blastn to map the marker sequences of cv. Morex genome to the scaffolds. Only uniquely mapped markers with coverage greater than 0.8 and identity greater than 0.95 were considered. To anchor the scaffolds to pseudochromosomes, ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biology 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1229">16</a></sup> was used (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Fig2">2a</a>). The synteny comparison between the newly assembled Tibetan hulless barley and barley cv. Morex (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Fig3">3a</a>) was performed using CoGe platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. The Plant Journal 53, 661â673 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1239">17</a></sup> (<a href="https://genomevolution.org/coge/">https://genomevolution.org/coge/</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0480-0/MediaObjects/41597_2020_480_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0480-0/MediaObjects/41597_2020_480_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="361" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The workflows of genome assembly and annotation used in this study. (<b>a</b>) Genome assembly pipeline; (<b>b</b>) Protein-coding gene annotation pipeline. Software and tools were indicated at lines, data and database information were shown in rectangles.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0480-0/MediaObjects/41597_2020_480_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0480-0/MediaObjects/41597_2020_480_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="607" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Genome comparison between Tibetan hulless barley and barley cv. Morex. (<b>a</b>) The plot shows the LAST alignments of predicted protein-coding genes in barley cv. Morex assembly and Tibetan hulless barley assembly. (<b>b</b>â<b>d</b>) Dot plots show the MUMMER alignments of Tibetan hulless barley scaffolds and assembled barley cv. Morex bacterial artificial chromosome sequences. Different coverages were shown in B (65%), C (85%) and D (99.9%). The differences can be due to either true sequence divergences or assembly errors.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Repetitive sequences annotation</h3><p>Repetitive DNA sequences are highly abundant in many organisms and their variations in abundancy resulted in remarkable genome size variations in plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Paterson, A. H. Leafing through the genomes of our major crop plants: strategies for capturing unique information. Nature Reviews Genetics 7, 174â184 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1287">18</a></sup>. In many Gramineae crop plants, repetitive elements represent more than 80% of their genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427â433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1291">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1294">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of wheat A subgenome Triticum urartu. Nature 557, 424â428 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1297">20</a></sup>. Repetitive elements can be classified as simple repeats and transposable elements (TE). Using tandem repeats finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Research 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1301">21</a></sup>, we annotated ~155âMb (3.89%) sequences as simple repeats. To annotate TE, we used both homology-based and <i>de novo</i> TE annotation tools: RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Current protocols in bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1â14.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1308">22</a></sup>, RepeatProteinMask<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Current protocols in bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1â14.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1313">22</a></sup>, RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>) and LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Research 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1321">23</a></sup>. For RepeatMasker, Repbase 21.01<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mobile DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1325">24</a></sup> was used. In total, ~87.5% of the assembled genome were identified as TE (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Tab4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-5"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Repetitive element annotation statistics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0/tables/5"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Protein-coding gene prediction</h3><p>For the annotation of protein-coding genes, we used a previously established gene annotation pipeline (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Fig2">2b</a>) with minor modifications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1473">4</a></sup>. For <i>de novo</i> gene prediction, we first extracted the full-length transcripts from the Iso-Seq data using SMRTLINK. In total, 39,442 full-length transcripts were obtained and were subsequently aligned to the assembled genome using GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859â1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1480">25</a></sup>. Among the 38,013 aligned transcripts, we removed all transcripts that had coverage less than 0.9 or sequence identity less than 0.85. This resulted in 14,099 high-quality full-length transcripts, which were used for open reading frame (ORF) prediction by TransDecoder (<a href="https://github.com/TransDecoder">https://github.com/TransDecoder</a>). In total, 13,936 (98.8%) transcripts contained at least one open reading frame (ORF) that is larger than 50 amino acids. These ORF containing transcripts were then assigned to 9,360 genes, which were considered as authentic genes. The authentic genes were then used for training the gene prediction models using AUGUSTUS v3.2.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, ii215âii225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1489">26</a></sup>. Based on the trained models, AUGUSTUS predicted 128,400 putative genes.</p><p>For homology based gene prediction, we used the protein sequences of seven monocot species (<i>Triticum urartu</i> (progenitor of wheat A genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of wheat A subgenome Triticum urartu. Nature 557, 424â428 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1499">20</a></sup><i>, Triticum tauscii</i> (progenitor of wheat D genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498â502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1505">27</a></sup>, <i>Brachy podiumdistachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Initiative, I. B. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1511">28</a></sup>, <i>Hordeum vulgare</i>8, <i>Oryza sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Du, H. et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nature Communications 8, 15324 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1521">29</a></sup>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="McCormick, R. F. et al. The Sorghum bicolor reference genome: improved assembly, gene annotations, a transcriptome atlas, and signatures of genome organization. The Plant Journal 93, 338â354 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1527">30</a></sup> and <i>Zea mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1533">31</a></sup>) from public databases. All protein sequences were aligned to the hulless barley genome using tblastn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. Journal of molecular biology 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1538">32</a></sup>. The gene structure was predicted using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Research 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1542">33</a></sup> with the input protein sequence as reference.</p><p>To provide further evidence for evaluating the predicted gene models, we assembled the transcriptome using available RNA-seq Illumina short-reads from different libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Wei, Z. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis revealed genes commonly responsive to varied nitrate stress in leaves of Tibetan hulless barley. Frontiers in Plant Science 7, 1067 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1549">34</a></sup>. The transcriptome was assembled using both reference-guided approach (mapping: hisat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nature Methods 12, 357â360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1553">35</a></sup>, assembly: stringtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nature Biotechnology 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1557">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nature Protocols 11, 1650â1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1560">37</a></sup>) and <i>de novo</i> approach (Trinity pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Grabherr, M. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nature Biotechnology 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1567">38</a></sup>). The reference-guided approach resulted in 47,490 transcripts and the <i>de novo</i> approach resulted in 722,803 transcripts.</p><p>The full-length transcripts from Iso-Seq, assembled transcripts from short-reads were used as evidence to evaluate the predicted gene models using EVidenceModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biology 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1578">39</a></sup>. For the data integration, evidence from different sources was assigned to different weight parameters: 20 for Iso-Seq assembly, 8 for short-reads assembly, 5 for homology-based prediction, 2 for AUGUSTUS gene prediction. In total, 129,269 transcripts were obtained, and the structural optimization was performed using PASA. We removed transcripts that either do not show any homology to sequences in nr or uniport database (blast results with identity &gt;â=â50% and coverage &gt;â=â50%) or have no protein sequences containing any Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bateman, A. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Research 32, D138âD141 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1582">40</a></sup> domain (hmmer results with e-valueâ&lt;â=â1e-5). For each gene, only the transcript with the longest protein sequences was kept. The tandem duplicated genes were identified using MCScanX. Genes (4,530) that contain large TE sequences (90% coverage) were discarded. The pipeline generated 61,303 genes.</p><p>We further classified these genes into high-confidence (HC) genes (40,457), which are likely true protein-coding genes, and less reliable low-confidence (LC) genes (20,846), which potentially are fragmented genes, pseudogenes and/or non-coding genes. This was done in a two-step procedure as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427â433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1590">8</a></sup>.</p><p>We annotated putative functions of the 61,303 protein sequences using public databases, including nr, KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M. &amp; Tanabe, M. KEGG for integration and interpretation of large-scale molecular data sets. Nucleic Acids Research 40, D109âD114 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1597">41</a></sup>, SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Research 31, 365â370 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1601">42</a></sup>, Trembl<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Research 31, 365â370 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1605">42</a></sup>, GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nature Genetics 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1609">43</a></sup>, PFAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bateman, A. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Research 32, D138âD141 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1613">40</a></sup>, and InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Mitchell, A. et al. The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years. Nucleic Acids Research 43, D213âD221 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1618">44</a></sup>. Blastp was used to compare the predicted protein sequences with the protein databases(e-value &lt;â=â1e-5). Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Conesa, A. &amp; Gotz, S. Blast2GO: a comprehensive suite for functional analysis in plant genomics. International Journal of Plant Genomics 2008, 619832, 12 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1622">45</a></sup> was used to annotate the GO terms using nr database (downloaded in December 2017) with default parameters. The protein domains were annotated using PfamScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Mistry, J., Bateman, A. &amp; Finn, R. D. Predicting active site residue annotations in the Pfam database. BMC Bioinformatics 8, 298 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1626">46</a></sup> and InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1630">47</a></sup> based on InterPro protein databases, including CATHGene3D<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Lees, J. G. et al. Gene3D: multi-domain annotations for protein sequence and comparative genome analysis. Nucleic Acids Research 42, D240âD245 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1634">48</a></sup>, HAMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Pedruzzi, I. et al. Nucleic Acids Research 41, D584âD589 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1638">49</a></sup>, PANTHER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Mi, H. et al. Nucleic Acids Research 41, D387âD395 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1672">57</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Non-coding gene prediction</h3><p>tRNAs were annotated using tRNAscan-SE v1.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Lowe, T. Nucleic Acids Research 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1684">58</a></sup> and rRNAs were annotated using blastn with the rRNA sequences from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i> (5S rRNA: AJ307354, 5.8S rRNA: AJ232900, 18S rRNA: X16077, 28S rRNA: AH001750). In addition, we also used INFERNAL to predict the miRNA and snRNA.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Data Records"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Data Records</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>The genomic Pacbio sequencing data (SRS3725794) and Iso-Seq sequencing data (SRS4809149) are available in NCBI Sequence Read Archive under SRP159129<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP159129&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1703">59</a></sup>. The available Illumina genome sequencing data that was deposited under SRP055042<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1707">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP055042&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1710">60</a></sup> was used in our genome assembly and validation processes. The final genome assembly and annotation was deposited at NCBI GenBank under SDOW00000000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Zeng, X. et al. Hordeum vulgare cultivar Lasa Goumang, whole genome shotgun sequencing project. Genbank, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:SDOW00000000&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1714">61</a></sup> and NCBI Assembly under GCA_004114815.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="NCBI Assembly, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.gca:GCA_004114815.1&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1718">62</a></sup>. The previously generated RNA sequencing data (deposited in NCBIâs Sequence Read Archive under SRP074870<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP074870&#xA;&#xA; (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1722">63</a></sup>) was used in our genome annotation steps. All the files in this project, such as the assembled scaffolds, repeat annotation, gene predictions and gene function annotations were uploaded to figshare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Xu, T. et al. An improved high-quality genome assembly and annotation of Tibetan hulless barley. Figshare, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.7582181.v5&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1727">64</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Technical Validation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Technical Validation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>We evaluated the quality of the new assembly using three independent approaches. First, we mapped 682.57âGb previously generated genomic Illumina paired-end reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zeng, X. et al. The draft genome of Tibetan hulless barley reveals adaptive patterns to the high stressful Tibetan Plateau. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America 112, 1095â1100 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1739">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP055042&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1742">60</a></sup> to the assembled genome. Overall, ~99.9% of paired-end reads were mapped to the genome concordantly. Second, using the mapped short reads, we estimated the quality value (QV) of the assembly using a previously described method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Xia, M. et al. Improved de novo genome assembly and analysis of the Chinese cucurbit Siraitia grosvenorii, also known as monk fruit or luo-han-guo. Gigascience 7, 6 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1746">65</a></sup> in which erroneous bases in the genome assembly were identified based on the variant calling software Genome Analysis Toolkit (GATK)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Heldenbrand, J. R. et al. Performance benchmarking of GATK3.8 and GATK4. Preprint at, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/348565v1&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1750">66</a></sup>. The estimated base pair error rate is 6.3E-06, suggesting high accuracy of the assembly at base-pair level. Third, we mapped available RNA-seq Illumina reads to the new assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Wei, Z. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis revealed genes commonly responsive to varied nitrate stress in leaves of Tibetan hulless barley. Frontiers in Plant Science 7, 1067 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1754">34</a></sup> using bowtie2 (parameters:--sensitiveÂ --score-min L,0,-0.1 -k 200Â --no-discordantÂ --gbar 99999999Â --dpad 0 -p 24Â --no-mixed -X 1000Â --mp 1,1 -I 1Â --np 1). The mapping rate with the new assembly (more than 73.6%) is more than 10% higher than the previous assembly. Fourth, we also mapped the PacBio long reads to the new assembly using blasr (parameters: -m 4Â --minMatch 8Â --minPctIdentity 70Â --bestn 1Â --nCandidates 20Â --maxScore -500). Overall, 91.2% PacBio long-reads can be mapped back to the assembly. Fifth, we downloaded the assembled bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) sequences from barley cv. Morex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="IBSC. (ed IPK Gatersleben) (Plant Genomics and Phenomics Research Data Repository (PGP), IPK Gatersleben, 2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1758">67</a></sup> and mapped them back to the new hulless barley assembly using mummer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biology 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1763">68</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#Fig3">3bâd</a>). Among 299 BACs that were larger than 200âkb and can be mapped back to barley cv. Morex, 74.6% showed high collinearity with the new hulless barley assembly (coverage greater than 60%). Sixth, we also used BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0480-0#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d42784e1770">69</a></sup> to assess the genome completeness. Among 1,440 conserved eukaryotic core genes, 1,378 (95.7%) were complete, 15 were fragmented and 47 were missing in the hulless barley genome assembly. Together, the results suggested that the newly assembled hulless barley genome is of high quality and will serve as a key resource for future research in barley genetics and genomics.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>The software mentioned in methods section are described blow.</p>
              <p><b>1) falcon</b>: version 1.2.4, parameters: (length_cutoff=5000, length_cutoff_pr=10000);</p>
              <p><b>2) pbalign</b>: contained in SMRT Link 4 toolkit, parameters:(--algorithm=blasr);</p>
              <p><b>3) variantCaller</b>: contained in SMRT Link 4 toolkit, parameters:(--algorithm=arrow);</p>
              <p><b>4) bowtie</b>: verion 1.1.2, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>5) SSPACE-STANDARD</b>: version 3.0, parameters:(-p 1 -g 2);</p>
              <p><b>6) SSPACE-LongRead</b>: version 1-1, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>7) blasr</b>: version 1.3.1.121193, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>8) GATK</b>: version 4.0.0.0, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>9) nucmer</b>: contained in mummer version 4.0.0beta2, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>10) BUSCO</b>: version 3, parameters:(-l embryophyta_odb9);</p>
              <p><b>11) Tandem repeats finder</b>: version 409, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>12) RepeatMasker</b>: version open-4.0.7, parameters: (-nolow -no_is -norna -engine ncbi -parallel 1);</p>
              <p><b>13) RepeatProteinMask</b>: version open-4.0.7, parameters:(-engine ncbi -noLowSimple -pvalue 0.0001);</p>
              <p><b>14) RepeatModeler</b>: version open-1.0.11, parameters:(-engine ncbi -pa 9);</p>
              <p><b>15) ltr_finder</b>: version 1.06, parameters:(-w 2);</p>
              <p><b>16) GMAP</b>: version 2017-05-08, parameters:(-z sense_force -f samse -n 0);</p>
              <p><b>17) TransDecoder</b>: version 4.0.1, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>18) augustus</b>: version 3.2.3, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>19) tblastn</b>: version 2.6.0+, parameters:(-evalue 1e-5 -seg no);</p>
              <p><b>20) GeneWise</b>: version 2.4.1, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>21) hisat2</b>: version 2.1.0, parameters:(--dtaÂ --no-discordantÂ --no-mixed);</p>
              <p><b>22) StringTie</b>: version 1.2.4, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>23) EVidenceModeler</b>: version 1.1.1, parameters:(--weights:PROTEIN GeneWise:5, TRANSCRIPT StringTie:8, ABINITIO_PREDICTION AUGUSTUS:2, OTHER_PREDICTION transdecoder:20);</p>
              <p><b>24) Trinity</b>: version 2.4.0, parameters: (--group_pairs_distance 500Â --path_reinforcement_distance 80Â --min_glue 3Â --min_kmer_cov 3Â --min_contig_length 100Â --KMER_SIZE 25Â --bflyHeapSpaceInit 1GÂ --bflyHeapSpaceMax 4GÂ --bfly_opts â-V 5Â --edge-thr=0.1Â --stderrâ);</p>
              <p><b>25) PASA</b>: version 2.1.0, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>26) MCScanX</b>: latest version, default parameters;</p>
              <p><b>27) tRNAscan-SE</b>: version 1.3.1, default parameters; 28) infernal: version 1.1.1, default parameters.</p>
              <p><b>28) tRNAscan-SE</b>: version 1.3.1, default parameters; 28) infernal: version 1.1.1, default parameters.</p>
